        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2debf4b2-0bd7-4b58-a191-1e66a4589d88'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d76731506" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>
Transition metal layered oxide materials with a general formula Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (M = Ni, Mn, Co, Fe) are widely researched with various possible electrode configurations. Herein, O3-type NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) layered oxide cathode materials are synthesized by solid-state reaction method, and its structural, thermal, and electrochemical performance in sodium ion battery is investigated. The structural analysis reveals that a single phase highly crystalline O3-type cathode material with an irregular particle shape is formed. The introduction of molybdenum improves the thermal stability of cathode materials, which can be attributed to the improved TMO<sub>2</sub> layers that provide stability to the material. The addition of Mo to Na-layered oxide cathode materials enhances electrochemical performance. The developed cathode materials, the NaNi<sub>0.475</sub>Mn<sub>0.475</sub>Mo<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, exhibit excellent specific discharge capacity (≈154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at C/20 rate, (an increase of ≈20% when compared to the NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) which can be attributed to the increased capacitance effect by the addition of Mo. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy study reveals that the diffusion of Na+ into/from the host structure is rapid during the first cycle and then gradually reduces with subsequent cycling due to the formation of the solid electrolyte interface layer, which hinders Na+ migration. This has a potential effect on the improved electrochemical performance of the material.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202300437-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202300437-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The world today requires efficient energy storage materials to resolve the current and anticipated energy crisis. Rechargeable batteries are excellent energy storage sources, and many significant research efforts have long been committed to enhancing the electrochemical properties of battery performance. The most widely studied batteries are lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), owing to their promising energy density and high voltage. However, a gradual decline in the resource availability of lithium (Li) and, thus, associated high costs render them unable to be deployed for large-scale applications. This limiting factor has directed the exploration of alternative resources to lithium, such as sodium, to develop sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The similar electrochemical behavior of sodium (Na) with competitive performance, resource abundance, and accompanying low cost has made Na a suitable alternative for Li.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0001_R_d76731497e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#ente202300437-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0006_R_d76731497e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Ceram. Int.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SIBs have the advantage of being safer than LIBs. However, as compared to LIBs, the SIBs suffer from low energy density. This stems from the fact that Na has a larger radius and heavier mass when compared to Li.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0007_R_d76731497e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This causes the retarded kinetics in SIBs and thus compels the SIBs to demonstrate lower electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, it is also reported that SIBs also suffer from the drawbacks of capacity degradation, poor rate performance, and low columbic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0008_R_d76731497e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Much research has been carried out to overcome the disadvantages associated with SIBs aiming for their commercialization. Various materials and structures have been developed employing different strategies in Na-based systems exhibiting comparable performance to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0008_R_d76731497e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0009_R_d76731497e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Ceram. Int.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the numerous proposed cathode materials for SIBs, layered oxide materials constitute an important class of materials. In terms of commercialization, this family of cathode materials presents a favorable and promising alternative to Li-layered oxides. Comparatively, the Na-layered oxide has a competitive performance with Li-layered oxides that pave the way for their easy transition to commercialization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0003_R_d76731497e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Technol.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na-layered oxides have a general form Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2,</sub> where 0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1 and TM =  Fe, Cr, Co, Mn, Ni, V, Cu, or mixtures of these transition metals. The Na-layered oxides are famous for their high capacities, high operating voltages, and simple synthesis procedures<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0010_R_d76731497e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> combined with excellent reversibility for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0011_R_d76731497e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The difference in the sizes of the Na<sup>+</sup> and the TM ions (Na<sup>+</sup> ion size &gt;TM ions) allows for the separation of the layers of the Na<sup>+</sup> and the TM layer. In comparison to Li systems, nearly all of the 3d TM elements are electrochemically active in layered Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0012_R_d76731497e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Select">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There can be single, binary, ternary, or even higher combinations of the TM elements in the layered oxide resulting in various compounds of Na-layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0012_R_d76731497e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Select">12</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0013_R_d76731497e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The various popular compounds from the single TM element are NaxCoO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0014_R_d76731497e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Synth. Met.">14</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0015_R_d76731497e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Solid State Ionics">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaxVO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0016_R_d76731497e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochem. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaCrO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0017_R_d76731497e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochem. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaTiO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0018_R_d76731497e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Inclusion Phenom.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaMnO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0019_R_d76731497e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. The Na-layered oxides compounds with the binary composition of TM are the O3-type and may be comprised of many formulations such as NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0020_R_d76731497e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The P2 type Na-layered oxides materials with ternary combinations of TM may also have compositions such as Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0021_R_d76731497e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0022_R_d76731497e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with generic formula of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>1−<i>y</i>-</sub>zFe<sub><i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0023_R_d76731497e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Electrochim. Acta">23</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0024_R_d76731497e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The family of Na-layered oxides can be classified into either P2, P3, or O3-types depending upon the position occupied by Na<sup>+</sup> between the TM oxide layers in a unit cell. In P2 and P3 Na-layered oxides, it occupies the prismatic position (P), and in the O3-type, it occupies the octahedral position (O).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0013_R_d76731497e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0025_R_d76731497e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The number of unique interlayers surrounded by oxide layers is represented by the preceding number. The Na content in the P2-type ranges from 0.6 to 0.9, whereas in O3-type, the Na content is close to 1. Examples of O3-types include O3-NaCoO<sub>2</sub>, O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2,</sub> etc. A manganese (Mn)-based Na-layered oxide cathode material has the advantages of being economical, environmentally friendly, and sustainable, at the same time offering high reversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0012_R_d76731497e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Select">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The distorted O3' Mn-containing NaMnO<sub>2</sub> delivers a high specific capacity of 185 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> in a voltage range of 2.0–3.8 V. It allows extraction of 0.8 Na per formula unit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0019_R_d76731497e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The first O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was reported by Komaba et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0020_R_d76731497e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is a promising cathode material owing to its high specific capacity value of &gt;120 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at a voltage range of 2.2–3.8 V. This was further analyzed by Qinjiang et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0026_R_d76731497e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> by synthesizing the same in an easy ethanol-mediated method. The material was synthesized with exposed {0 1 0} facets and hollow structures. The specific capacity achieved was 133 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> with improved rate capability. A concerning drawback associated with Mn-based Na-layered oxides is the Jahn Teller distortion introduced by the Mn<sup>3+</sup> ion and the phase changes caused by the larger ionic radius of the Na ion. This causes degradation of specific capacity and results in poor rate capability performance when subjected to repeated sodium intercalation/deintercalation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0027_R_d76731497e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The heteroatom modification strategy is an effective way to improve the shortcomings in SIBs. The heteroatom modification improves the structure's stability and the adjustment of Mn oxidation states to suppress the unstable Mn<sup>3+</sup> transition. Together with this, the heteroatom modification enhances the electronic and ionic conductivities of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0028_R_d76731497e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources">28</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0029_R_d76731497e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mater. Chem. A">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A Mo doping together with Mn, combining the synergetic effect of the low-valence Mn<sup>2+</sup> and the high-valence Mo<sup>6+</sup> has been carried out by Meghnani et al.,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0030_R_d76731497e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ChemPhysChem">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in the Na-based battery material Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, wherein the substitution happened in the V locations, and the substitution resulted in the enhanced diffusion of the Na<sup>+</sup> brought about by the larger interstitial spacings from the addition of Mo.</p>
         
         <p>The heteroatom modification in O3-type Na-layered oxide cathode material was carried out earlier. The molybdenum (Mo) doping in P2 type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>1−<i>x</i></sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode was carried out by Zhang et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0011_R_d76731497e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mo doping paved the way for increasing the Na interlayer distance and improved the MnO<sub>2</sub> slabs. The increased interlayer spacing enhances the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, and the stability of the structure is improved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0011_R_d76731497e762" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This was the inspiration to synthesize the Mo-doped O3-type NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05,0.1) material through a conventional solid-state synthesis route to obtain higher crystallinity of the structure. To the best of our knowledge, a Mo-doped O3-type NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05,0.1) material has not been reported and analyzed so far for its structural and electrochemical performance. It is noticed that the Mo doping has resulted in the ability to improve the specific capacity to 152 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>. The capacity retention obtained was 41% for 20 cycles at 0.05C. It is also found that the amount of Mo dopant has a significant influence on the electrochemical performance of O3-type NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>. A detailed comparative analysis of structural, thermal, and electrochemical behavior of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05,0.1) is investigated to elucidate the role of Mo addition in modifying the properties of O3-type NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202300437-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202300437-sec-0002-title">2 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202300437-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ente202300437-sec-0003-title">2.1 Material Synthesis</h3>
            
            <p>The NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) cathode materials containing various amounts of Mo were prepared by a solid-state synthesis method. The reagents used were sodium acetate (CH<sub>3</sub>COONa) (Sigma Aldrich), MoO<sub>3</sub> (Sigma Aldrich), manganese oxalate (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) (Alfa Aesar), and nickel acetate (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>6</sub>NiO<sub>4</sub>) (Alfa Aesar). The stoichiometric amounts of the reagents were added along with ethanol and were ball-milled at 300 rpm for 10 h. To compensate for potential Na loss during high-temperature sintering, a 10% surplus of CH<sub>3</sub>COONa was employed. The mixture was dried, pelletized, and then sintered in the presence of air in a muffle furnace (Nabertherm 3000) at 950 °C for 12 h to obtain the phase pure materials. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0001">1</a> depicts the synthesis steps for the production of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) cathode materials. The materials with various stoichiometry values: NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0), NaNi<sub>0.475</sub>Mn<sub>0.475</sub>Mo<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05), and NaNi<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>Mo<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.1) are termed as NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1, respectively, throughout the document.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c71db49f-2289-42b2-9df7-c155a0350335/ente202300437-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c71db49f-2289-42b2-9df7-c155a0350335/ente202300437-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18ea7ff5-6654-4dab-ad20-a93885a83c55/ente202300437-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c71db49f-2289-42b2-9df7-c155a0350335/ente202300437-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the synthesis of Mo-doped sodium-layered oxide material.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To confirm the crystal structure and to ascertain the phase purity of synthesized NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1), the materials were analyzed with X-ray diffractometer (XRD) using PANalytical diffractometer, Almelo. The XRD analysis was conducted at room temperature using Cu-Kα radiation (1.5425 A°) with 2° per minute as the scan rate with a scan range from 10° to 90°(10 ≤ 2<i>θ</i> ≤ 90), and with a step size of 0.01313°. To analyze the particle morphology and elemental composition of the developed materials, field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) paired with energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) (Nova NanoSEM 450 with Bruker EDX Detector, FEI) was used. The magnification scale was from 35× to 1000 000×. To get an insight into the structural features of the prepared materials, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) was carried out using 200 kV TEM, TECNAI G2, TEM TF 20, and FEI. The thermal stability of the synthesized material was conducted using a TGA analyzer (Pyris 4000, PerkinElmer- USA). Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy was used to confirm the presence of the various functional groups in the synthesized material. The FTIR instrument from PerkinElmer, USA, was used, and the spectra were recorded from 4000 to 500 cm<sup>−1</sup> at a spectral resolution of 4.0 cm<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>To prepare the electrodes, a slurry was first prepared by mixing the active material, polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), and conductive acetylene black in the ratio of 80:10:10, respectively, in a suitable quantity of n-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP). The as-prepared slurry was kept for stirring for 12 h, and then it was then coated onto battery-grade Al foil with the help of a doctor's blade. The electrode sheets were dried by transferring them to a vacuum drying oven at 120 °C for 6 h. The electrode sheets were then calendered using a rolling press. The electrodes of diameter 14 mm were punched out from the calendered electrode sheets. They were dried once again to remove any moisture by transferring them to a vacuum oven at 120 °C for 2 h. The electrodes were transferred to an argon (Ar) filled glove box. The mass loading of the electrodes was calculated to be 1.58–2.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Finally, the CR 2032-coin cells were assembled in the glove box using NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) as an electrolyte, prepared active materials as cathode, and sodium metal as an anode. Borosilicate glass fiber (Whatman GF/D) was used as the separator. The cells were then subjected to extensive electrochemical testing.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202300437-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202300437-sec-0004-title">3 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Mo-modified material takes the form of the O3-type Na layered oxide, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0002">2a</a>. In the O3-type layered oxide, the stacking follows the sequence ABCABC, and the Na occupies the O3 sites between the TM layers<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0031_R_d76731497e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XRD patterns of the synthesized active material NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 is shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0002">2b</a>. The major peaks of the active material with Mo addition are indexed to the reference pdf file number 98-023-6350, corresponding to the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. All the samples crystallize in the hexagonal structure with an R-3 m space group. The Mo peaks are clearly seen in the <i>x</i> = 0.05 and <i>x</i> = 0.1 samples at 27.63°. The characteristic peaks of the NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 indicated by the (111), (022), and (113) at 2<i>θ</i> values of 16.84°, 27.6°, and 32.58°, respectively, confirm the presence of Mo in the material. They represent the presence of the Mo-containing Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub> (pdf card- 98-004- 4523) material in a cubic structure. These findings are consistent with the previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0027_R_d76731497e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0032_R_d76731497e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Alloys Compd.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e88f71b-0496-4be4-a46b-114cb89d48dc/ente202300437-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e88f71b-0496-4be4-a46b-114cb89d48dc/ente202300437-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/09833dcd-1a8c-458c-a39f-491c8709c346/ente202300437-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e88f71b-0496-4be4-a46b-114cb89d48dc/ente202300437-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Crystal structure of the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.475</sub>Mn<sub>0.475</sub>Mo<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMM-0.05). b) A comparison of XRD spectra of the synthesized materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>

         <p>It is seen that Mo addition has resulted in an increase of <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> axes, while axes <i>c</i> is reduced. This is also confirmed by the reduced <i>d</i>-spacing obtained from the HRTEM images. The higher the concentration of Mo, the higher the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> axes values and the reduced <i>c</i> value. This is attributed to the larger atomic radii of Mo when compared to Ni and Mn. The diffraction peaks have been shifted in the 2<i>θ</i> angles in accordance with the change in the lattice parameters. There is no change in the position of the highest intensity peak in all three samples. There is a peak shift at other 2<i>θ</i> values observed in the samples. The NNMM-0.05 and NNMM-0.1 peaks are shifted in both directions. Specifically, the peaks at 16.63° shifted to the left, the peak at 33.74 was shifted to the left, and the peak at higher angles was shifted to the right. The presence of low-angle peaks shifted toward the left indicates an expansion of the lattice, brought about by adding Mo. However, the shift at higher angles could possibly be from the interstitial defects introduced in the crystal lattice. The same fact has also been reported somewhere in the study of Darga et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0003_R_d76731497e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Technol.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The particle size and morphology of the material were analyzed from the FE-SEM images. FE-SEM images of the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 cathode materials containing various amounts of Mo are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0003">3a–c</a>. Overall, all the materials exhibit irregular and nonuniform particle morphology, as shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0003">3a–c</a>, which is attributed to the solid-state synthesis method. The NNMM-0.0 material (without any Mo addition) shows a particle size of ≈0.7–1.75 μm, while the NNMM-0.05 (material containing 0.063 wt% Mo) and the NNMM-0.1 (material containing 0.12 wt% Mo) indicate a particle size of ≈1–3 and 0.9–1.57 μm, respectively. Some particles of the NNMM-0.1 particles show a size of around 5 μm. The morphology is nonuniform in nature. The NNMM-0.1 indicates a distorted structure when compared to the NNMM-0.0 and NNMM-0.05. Mo being larger in size, the distortion stems from this large size of Mo and it can be further noticed that NNMM-0.05 and NNMM-0.1 show increased hollow spaces when compared to the NNMM-0.0 sample, which facilitates the easy diffusion of ions in the structure. A comparison of the elemental mapping images of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) cathode materials containing various amounts of Mo is shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0003">3d–i</a>. The elemental mapping of the samples confirms the presence of Mo in the NNMM-0.5 and NNMM-0.1.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1003e14d-a05d-4127-92ac-cfbdbe09cad8/ente202300437-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1003e14d-a05d-4127-92ac-cfbdbe09cad8/ente202300437-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/67588efa-3db7-4b38-8f7e-a246c1fcea54/ente202300437-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1003e14d-a05d-4127-92ac-cfbdbe09cad8/ente202300437-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">FE-SEM images of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) cathode materials containing various amount of Mo. a) NNMM-0.0, b) NNMM-0.05, c) NNMM-0.1, and d–i) elemental mapping of NNMM-0.05.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The transmission electron microscopy of the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 cathode materials containing various amount of Mo are presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0004">4a–c</a>. All the materials exhibit irregular and nonuniform particle morphology consistent with FE-SEM images (Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0003">3</a>). Similarly, the high-resolution transmission emission (HR-TEM) images of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1) cathode materials are also shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0004">4a–c</a> and the lattice structure images are shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0004">4d–f</a>. The <i>d</i>-spacing has been calculated from the HRTEM images using Image J software and is shown in Figure (g–i). The <i>d</i> spacing values for the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 actually decrease from 0.65, 0.249, and 0.20 nm, respectively. It is further noticed that the <i>d</i> spacings of the developed materials are consistent with the reference 98-023-6350 for the O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The lattice fringes exhibit random patterns indicating a crystalline poly structure which was also confirmed by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns, as shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0004">4j–l</a>. Moreover, the SAED patterns for the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.10 indicate Mo addition does not alter the crystal structure of the material significantly owing to its small amount. A comparison of SAED images suggests that the NNMM-0.0 shows very clear rings, whereas the Mo addition has resulted in slightly distorted rings. The structure of all the developed materials is polycrystalline as indicated by the presence of bright spots in a circular pattern.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0033_R_d76731497e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de8cfd91-ddcd-4248-b2ef-c2c41aba65cf/ente202300437-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/de8cfd91-ddcd-4248-b2ef-c2c41aba65cf/ente202300437-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/377c8b64-151d-4a99-b8b1-51d953cc9e19/ente202300437-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de8cfd91-ddcd-4248-b2ef-c2c41aba65cf/ente202300437-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) TEM image analysis of NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1, d–f) lattice fringes in NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1, g–i) <i>d</i> spacing of the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1, and j–l) SAED images of NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The presence of the Mo bonding in the synthesized material is also confirmed by carrying out Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy. The FTIR results for the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0005">5a</a>. The absorption bands in the FTIR spectra in the region 850–1000 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to various Mo-O bonds, including Mo<sup>6+</sup>, Mo<sup>5+</sup>, and Mo<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0034_R_d76731497e1471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0035_R_d76731497e1471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bridge oxygen symmetry stretching mode of Mo-O-Mo is at 872 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0034_R_d76731497e1478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The addition of Mo has resulted in the shift of the peak from 879 to 864 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 835 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The vibrational peaks between 1000 and 900 cm<sup>−1</sup> indicate the Mo = O characteristic stretching of the hexagonal phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0036_R_d76731497e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Catal. Sci. Technol.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bands at 1422 are characteristic of the acetate (COO) ions<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0037_R_d76731497e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37">37</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0038_R_d76731497e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Appl. Polym. Sci.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the intensity of the peak at 1422 cm<sup>−1</sup> is decreased with Mo doping, indicating the bonds’ weakening due to the Mo addition.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d300dc3-bb30-4e27-9278-9f1cc70f1a69/ente202300437-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d300dc3-bb30-4e27-9278-9f1cc70f1a69/ente202300437-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e8cf83a5-8afc-419a-bb86-7b57492df20a/ente202300437-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d300dc3-bb30-4e27-9278-9f1cc70f1a69/ente202300437-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FTIR and b) XPS analysis indicating the Mo valence c) TGA analysis of NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>An X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy was carried out to identify the valence states of the Mo incorporation in NNMM-0.05 and the results of the peak fitting of the XPS data are shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0005">5b</a>. The peak fitting was done using XPS peak 41 software and the fitted curve indicates the Mo(0), MoO<sub>2</sub>, and MoO<sub>3</sub> in the sample. The MoO<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub> represent the Mo<sup>+4</sup> and Mo<sup>+6</sup> redox species. The peaks of the Mo<sup>+4</sup> 3<i>d</i><sub>5/2</sub> and 3<i>d</i><sub>3/2</sub> occur at 230.69 and 234.11 eV respectively, while the Mo<sup>+6</sup> 3<i>d</i><sub>5/2</sub> and 3<i>d</i><sub>3/2</sub> occur at 232.23 and 235.79 eV. These values match with previously reported works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0011_R_d76731497e1563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0039_R_d76731497e1563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 228.04 is indicative of Mo(0) in the sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0040_R_d76731497e1569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results confirm the sample has Mo in Mo(0), Mo<sup>+4</sup>, and Mo<sup>+6</sup> states, which matches with the FTIR analysis.</p>
         
         <p>Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) is conducted to analyze the thermal stability of the developed materials, and the results are shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0005">5c</a>. The materials in general exhibit a step-like graph, which is representative of a single stage of weight loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0041_R_d76731497e1584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Ind. Eng. Chem.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NNMM-0.0 indicates a very slight increase in the mass from the oxidation products during the heating, which then follows a decreasing trend indicating weight loss. The NNMM-0.05 and NNMM-0.1 also show very slight increases in mass but are then followed by a linear decrease in mass. The materials do not show any significant reduction in weight up to a temperature of 450 °C, confirming their excellent thermal stability. A significant weight loss is observed after a temperature of 600 °C. Compared to the NNMM-0.0 and NNMM-0.05, the NNMM-0.1 has shown less degradation throughout the entire range of studied temperature range. This indicates the Mo addition has enhanced the material's thermal stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202300437-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ente202300437-sec-0005-title">3.1 Electrochemical Characterization Results</h3>
            
            <p>
               <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the galvanostatic charge–discharge of the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1 at a 0.05C rate in the voltage range of 1.5–4.5 V. The discharge capacity obtained is 105, 129, and 154 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> with <i>x</i> = 0, 0.05, and 0.1, respectively, in NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05, 0.1). The highest discharge capacity is noticed at <i>x</i> = 0.05 Mo addition. The high value of discharge capacity obtained from NNMM-0.05 is very promising and is a remarkable value in the SIB. However, further addition of Mo (<i>x</i> = 0.10) causes a reduction in discharge capacity due to the electrochemically inactive nature of Mo. The reason for this is that O<sub>2</sub> cannot form hybrid orbitals with Mo and Mo has empty <i>d</i> [4<i>d</i><sup>0</sup>] orbitals. The orbit hybridization can happen between O<sub>2</sub> and Mn only. The limited charge delocalization only on O<sub>2</sub> and Mn and the increased charge density on O<sub>2</sub> results in limited redox reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0011_R_d76731497e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0042_R_d76731497e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem.">42</a>, <a href="#ente202300437-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0043_R_d76731497e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the best performance, the electrochemical behavior of NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.05,0.1) cathodes at <i>x</i> = 0.05 is explored further in our current study. The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves show many redox reaction-based plateaus and curves, indicating the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and phase transitions occurring with the MO<sub>2</sub> slab gliding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0025_R_d76731497e1674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reversible phase transitions occur from the O3 to the P3 as is evident in the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> without breaking Ni<span class="icomoon"></span>O and Mn—O bonds. The extraction of 0.1–0.2 Na<sup>+</sup> ions has an extended curve up to 3.375 V in the new material as compared to a value of around 2.5 V in the earlier reported work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0020_R_d76731497e1688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The extraction of the Na<sup>+</sup> in the voltage range of 1.5–3.37 V takes place without any phase transition which is possible by the Mo addition. Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the first, 10<sup>th</sup>, and 50<sup>th</sup> charge/discharge cycles of NNMM-0.05 when cycled between 1.5–4.5 V. A reduction in charge/discharge capacity is noticed with the increasing number of cycles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa50953d-dc3a-4ec8-bfb0-33a7958a0bae/ente202300437-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa50953d-dc3a-4ec8-bfb0-33a7958a0bae/ente202300437-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1802478f-8094-4c7a-875c-31139e116815/ente202300437-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa50953d-dc3a-4ec8-bfb0-33a7958a0bae/ente202300437-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the NNMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05, and NNMM-0.1, b) galvanostatic charge–discharge profile of NNMM-0.05 for the 1st cycle, 10th cycle, 50th cycle in the voltage window of 1.5–4.0 V, c) galvanostatic charge-discharge profile for the NNMM-0.05 between 1.5 and 4.0 V, and d) GITT curve for the NNMM-0.05 material.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The material shows capacity degradation, and this is a predominant character of the O3-type materials at high voltages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0013_R_d76731497e1728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This occurs mainly because of the difficulty of extracting Na ions at high voltages, which is also prevalent in O3-type Na-layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0044_R_d76731497e1733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Energy Chem.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This happens due to the irreversible structural changes taking place and the coinsertion of electrolyte solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0045_R_d76731497e1738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a complete comparison, the charge/discharge behavior of NNMM-0.05 between 1.5 and 4.0 V is also presented in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0006">6d</a>. An improvement in capacity retention with successive cycling is evident. This is in alignment with earlier studies done with a voltage range from 2.2 to 3.8 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0020_R_d76731497e1747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Inorg. Chem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis and performance evaluation is done for all three cathodes (NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05,0.1), however, owing to the best discharge capacity available from NNMM-0.05, it is chosen alone to explore its electrochemical characterization further.</p>
            
            <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) technique for the NNMM-0.05 was carried out to understand the thermodynamic behavior and the kinetics of the reactions involved. The GITT analysis was carried out at a C rate of 0.1C. The charging curve exhibits high polarization from 0 V to around 3.0 V and after this, the polarization is drastically reduced. This represents good working conditions in the voltage from 3.25 to 3.75 V. And during the discharging, very low polarization is visible in the region 3.5 V to around 2.5 V.</p>
            
            <p>The cyclic voltammetry curve (CV) for the NNMM-0.05 is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0007">7a</a>. The Na ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) extraction during the oxidation is visible. The complete extraction of one Na<sup>+</sup> takes place at the voltage range of 4.0 V and above. The intermediate phase transitions occurring at lower voltages are indicated by the low and minor peaks in the CV curve. The CV curve shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0007">7b</a> gives a comparison of the CV of the NNMM-0.0 and NNMM-0.05 and NNMM-0.1. It can be seen that the polarization is less in NNMM-0.05 as compared to NNMM-0.0 as there is a slight left shift in the oxidation peak of the CV curve of the NNMM-0.05 when compared to the NNMM-0.0.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0046_R_d76731497e1790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks in the CV curve of the NNMM-0.1 are less pronounced which indicates the redox activity is poor in comparison to the NNMM-0.05. This arises from the disordered lattice due to the high Mo addition. This indicates the higher concentration of Mo impairs the redox activity of the layered oxide, which highlights the fact that the electrochemical activity of the NNMM-0.05 is the most predominant.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4d3787e-ad3e-48ad-918d-b043fdf7648c/ente202300437-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4d3787e-ad3e-48ad-918d-b043fdf7648c/ente202300437-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/df8a72b4-b50e-4865-be11-e964b4f9085d/ente202300437-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4d3787e-ad3e-48ad-918d-b043fdf7648c/ente202300437-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curve of the NNMM-0.05 for the 1<sup>st</sup> and 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle, b) CV curve of the NMM-0.0, NNMM-0.05 and NNMM-0.1, c) rate capability of the NNMM-0.05, and d) capacity retention curve of the NNMM-0.05.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The rate capability (RC) curve of the NNMM-0.05 material is shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0007">7c</a>. The various C rates used for the RC curve include 0.1, 0.5, 1, and 2C, and the resulting specific discharge capacities of 162, 80, 54, 30.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. About ≈67% of the capacity was maintained at a discharge rate of 0.1C, which is fairly good. The NNMM-0.05 material exhibits a columbic efficiency of nearly 80% when considering 50 cycles, as shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0007">7d</a>. The capacity is seen to be fading during the cycling, which is a characteristic of O3-type layered materials, and is found to be ≈41.23% after 20 cycles and ≈13.3% for 50 cycles, as shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0007">7d</a>. This requires further optimization studies to use suitable materials that can enhance cyclability features.</p>
            
            <p>The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted for the NNMM-0.05 material at 0.1C for 30 cycles and are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0008">8a</a>. The EIS tests were carried out prior to cycling and after the first cycle, 10<sup>th</sup> cycle, 20<sup>th</sup> cycle, and 30<sup>th</sup> cycle. The EIS resulting data were then fitted using RelaxIS software. The distance on the real axis up to the point where the semicircle begins represents the bulk resistance of the system. The bulk resistance arising from the internal resistance of the cell<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202300437-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202300437-bib-0047_R_d76731497e1853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Electrochem. Sci. Technol.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> is present in all cycles, with a value of 57.6 Ω for the uncycled cell. The value increases to 68.7 Ω after the first cycle, which is then increased to 70, 90, and 100.5 Ω after 10, 20, and 30 cycles, respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0008">8b</a>, the first semicircle represents the impedance caused by the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, and the EIS plots confirm that the impedance increases as the layer becomes thicker as the diffusion progresses. The second semicircle represents the charge transfer resistance which is associated with the kinetics of the reaction and increases as the cycling progresses. The charge transfer resistance linked to the reaction kinetics is sensitive to phase transitions occurring and particle size. The system, as represented by the EIS curve, indicates semi-infinite linear diffusion because of the presence of the Warburg impedance lines. From the curves, the diffusion is indicated to be higher for the first cycle and reduced upon cycling. This occurs due to the SEI layer formation, which hinders the Na<sup>+</sup> from the host structure. The diffusion coefficient of Mo containing NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> when compared to the pristine material was established in previous studies which were of the order of 10<sup>−12</sup>. The fitted results of the EIS were analyzed, and the fitted data and raw data follow an exact match, as indicated by Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0008">8c</a>. The equivalent circuit used for fitting the experimental data is shown in Figure <a href="#ente202300437-fig-0008">8d</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202300437-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5f5c05ad-7aaa-45a1-9a56-0368330419bc/ente202300437-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5f5c05ad-7aaa-45a1-9a56-0368330419bc/ente202300437-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e4777b4-3ea6-4d37-afde-278721d4a5e1/ente202300437-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5f5c05ad-7aaa-45a1-9a56-0368330419bc/ente202300437-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202300437-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202300437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) EIS spectra of the NNMM-0.05 before cycling, after 1<sup>st</sup> cycle, and after 10, 20, and 30 cycles. b) EIS spectra depicting the <i>R</i><sub>b</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>SEI,</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the NNMM-0.05 material, c) the raw data and the fit data comparison, and d) the equivalent circuit used for data fitting.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202300437-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202300437-sec-0006-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>NaNi<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mn<sub>(1−<i>x</i>)/2</sub>Mo<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.05, 0.1) O3-type Na-layered oxide cathode materials were developed by solid-state reaction method and its structural, thermal, and electrochemical characterization was investigated. The addition of Mo to Na-layered oxide cathode materials influences their electrochemical performance. As a comparison, among all the developed cathode materials, the NaNi<sub>0.475</sub>Mn<sub>0.475</sub>Mo<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibited excellent specific discharge capacity (≈154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at C/20, which can be attributed to the increased capacitance effect brought about by Mo addition. However, further addition of Mo (<i>x</i> = 0.10) causes a reduction in discharge capacity due to the electrochemically inactive nature of Mo. The reason for this is that O<sub>2</sub> cannot form hybrid orbitals with Mo and Mo has empty <i>d</i> [4<i>d</i><sup>0</sup>] orbitals. NaNi<sub>0.475</sub>Mn<sub>0.475</sub>Mo<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> does not show pronounced improvement in rate capability and capacity retention and thus needs to be improved using dopants such as silver (Ag). The GITT analysis presents good kinetics and the EIS analysis confirms the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> into/from the host structure is fastest during the first cycle which gradually decreases with successive cycling due to the formation of the SEI layer, which hinders the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

            <div class="accordion__content" style="display: none;" role="region" aria-labelledby="references-section-1" id="ente202300437-bibl-0001">
